Michael J. Ewedosh
University Student 
Ambassadors
Would you like to:
- Have dinner with Carnegie Mellon University
Trustees
- Give a campus tour to the Israeli ambassador
- Meet U.S. Attorney General Dick Thornburgh
- Attend a reception with Peter Ueberroth
These are just some of the advantages of being 
a University Student Ambassador. To find out 
more about the program, plan to attend one of the 
informational meetings. They are scheduled for 
Thursday, November 9 in Skibo Room 13 and 
Wednesday, November 15 in the Faculty Lounge, 
Skibo at 6 p.m.
Requirements:
- Full time undergraduate student
- Q.P.A. of at least 2.5
- Outstanding communication and leadership skills
Application forms are available at the Skibo 
Information Desk, the Office of Special Events at 
3030 Hamburg Hall and at the University Relations 
Office at 5017 Forbes Avenue. Applications must 
be returned to the Office of Special Events, 3030 
Hamburg Hall, no later than 5 p.m., Friday 
November 17.
The University Student Ambassador program is sponsored 
by the University Relations Division.

FORUM
The Ball makes a comeback
In last Februarys icy rain, sixty 
CFA students stood outside 
Warner Hall and protested the 
cancellation of one of Carnegie 
Mellons oldest traditions, the 
Beaux Arts Ball.
 The Beaux Arts Ball, legend tells 
us, is a once-every-four-years, allnight extravaganza open only to 
CFA students and their dates. 
Apparently, the 1989 Ball was 
cancelled because things got just 
a little out of hand during the 
1985 Ball, and the CFA building 
;, sustained a great deal of damage.
After many plans and meetings, 
the 1989 Ball was revived, post
poned, moved off campus, and fi
nally cancelled due to lack of in
terest. It seems students didnt 
want to go to a Ball held anywhere 
but in CFA.
Well, the tradition is back. Un
willing to let a good thing die, a 
committee of CFA students is 
meeting with administrators, 
trying to find ways around the 
objections. Tentatively, the Ball 
will be held in early 1990  and in 
the CFA building, where it be
longs.
But this years committee is 
doing more than just mounting 
another attempt to push through 
the same old idea. The students 
have drastically restructured the 
Ball, returning its focus to its 
historical roots as a celebration of
CFAs creativity and ingenuity.
To answer the administrations 
objections to the 1989 Ball, the 
students are taking steps to elimi
nate the damage to the CFA build
ing caused during 1985s Ball, and 
have moved to prohibit alcohol at 
the event.
What impresses us, however, is 
the incredible amount of energy 
and enthusiasm the Balls sup
porters are using to generate interest. The tremendous banners 
hanging along Margaret Morri
son and Skibo are matched by the 
colorful posters in buildings in 
which CFA students take classes.
The Balls organizers didnt want 
to talk to the press about their 
plans until they received a final 
approval from Warner Hall, and 
we respect their intentions. In
stead of an article this week about 
the plans for the 1990 Ball, well 
content ourselves to cheer them 
on, and report it next week.
Hats off, too, to CFA Dean Lowry 
Burgess. In his first year on the 
job hes showing that students are 
a priority  a pretty unfamiliar 
idea in CFA.
Its going to take a lot of organi
zation and publicity to overcome 
the inertia remaining after last 
years fiasco. But, goes the old 
wisdom, excitement is infectious 
 and were getting pretty psy
ched for 1990.
Tls,gl i4u b4turlMi nv4tI IPl OzK
When most companies announce 
that they are recruiting at CMU, 
students get excited because they 
want to work for them.
The Central Intelligence Agency 
was no exception. However, when 
the CIA announced that it would 
recruit here, some students be
came excited notonly because they 
dont want to work for it, but they 
dont want anyone else to, either.
More than 30 protestors stood in 
front of Warner Hall Thursday 
and called upon the University to 
deny the CIA the privilege to re
cruit on campus. They also handed 
out educational pamphlets and 
made speeches about past deeds 
of the intelligence agency.
We are not about to pass judge
ment on the CIA and the legiti
macy of its operations. However, 
the CIA has as much right to re
cruit at Carnegie Mellon as antiSEI protestors, Lyndon LaRouche 
supporters, and evangelists have 
to speak on the Cut.
There is a not-so-fine line be-Ha -ve-vd

by a University policy.

lot of energy into educating stu
dents about the CIAs alleged 
misdeeds, no one would apply for 
jobs with them. Then the CIA 
would be unlikely to waste its 
money by coming back to CMU.
At least that would be the deci
sion of the individual students 
and the CIA  and not of the 
crowd.

All students (except CIT/MCS freshmen) are expected to register during the week of November 6-10 if they wish to enroll at Carnegie Mellon 
for Spring 1990. If you wont be returning for reasons other than graduation, submit a completed Application for Withdrawal or Leave of 
Absence (clearly marked "Spring 1990") to the Registrar's Office, Warner Hall 201.
You will use the new Bio Sheet to update demographic information and the new Registration/Add/Drop form to register for courses-- the 33D 
is gone! Bio Sheets are mailed to each student; Registration/Add/Drop forms are inside each Schedule of Classes. CIT/MCS freshmen have 
received separate instructions and do not register at this time. By October 26, the Spring 1990 Schedule of Classes will be available at the
Skibo Information Desk, outside the CFA Dean's Office and inside the main entrance to Baker Hall. _______ _______
REGISTRATION INSTRUCTIONS*****
1. Pick up a Spring 1990 Schedule of Classes with Registration/Add/Drop form inside (forms are also available at each department).
2. Plan course selections and alternatives with your advisor, and obtain your advisor's approval on the Registration/Add/Drop form. Many advisors will extend 
office hours during Registration; contact yours for specific approval information. Registration/Add/Drop forms are not valid without advisor approval.
3. On your registration day (or any day after, during the week of Nov. 6-10), between 9:30 am and 4:00 pm, go to the departments teaching the course(s) in 
which you wish to register. The department will enter the course(s) into your schedule on the Student Information System and sign approval on the 
Registration/Add/Drop form. Remember that if you are on a "wait list", your are not registered for the course; you must add the course later (if approved).
4. After registering for all courses, return the Registration/Add/Drop form to your department. The goldenrod copy is yours to keep.
5. Approximately one week after Registration, you will receive confirmation of your course schedule in the mail._______________________________________________
P.S. Your department may conduct a "pre-registration" prior to Registration Week. They'll send you instructions if necessary.

Stewart Levine is a second-year. 
MSIAcandidatein GSIA who be-~ 
lieves that Monroeville should 
have been Most Livable City.
~l may be an art major, 
but I know 
a little something 
about economics.
Youve done your home
work. You know where the 
best values are. You also 
know that with AT&T, it costs 
less than most people think 
to stay in touch with your 
family and friends.
In fact, you can make a 
10-minute, coast-to-coast 
call any time, any day for less 
than $3.00 with AT&T.*And 
who else can promise imme
diate credit for wrong num
bers, the fastest connections, 
and the largest worldwide 
long distance network?
Nobody but AT&T.
For more information 
on AT&T Long Distance 
Products and Services, like 
the AT&T Card, call 1 800 
525-7955, Ext. 100.
*Adtl applicable taxes and surchan>es.

COURSE: NAI.URE_Q.FJLAtiG.UAGE(8Q:3 80)
TIME: MWF 10:30-11:20
PLACE: PH 125B
INSTRUCTOR: STAFF
COURSE DESCRIPTION:
This course is designed as a broad introduction to linguistic phenomena, 
epecialiy natural language (human speech") and its properties. Besides 
surveying a variety of data, the course will provide the student with an 
appreciation of the principal components of linguistic analysis, including 
aspects of phonetics, phonology, morphology, syntax, semantics, and 
pragmatics.
The topics that will be discussed include language acquisition, 
relations between language and thought, the biological bases of human 
language, animal systems of communication, aspects of non-verbal 
communication, bilingualism, pidgins and creoles, and sociolinguistics.
COURSE REQUIREMENTS;
. Two midterms and one final exam 
. Occasional written homework assignments
COURSE: INTRO . TO LOGIC -SELF-PACED (80-2101
TIME: WEDS. JAN. 17TH( 4-6 P.M.)
PLACE: PH 126A
INSTRUCTOR: RICHARD SCHEINES
COURSE DESCRIPTION:
This is a self-paced, computer-taught introduction to logic. The course covers 
sentential and full first order predicate logic, as well as applications to intreger 
arithmetic, probability theory, and social choice theory. There are only two 
regular class meetings; an introduction at the beginning of the term and a final 
exam. Three scheduled help sessions are held every week for those who want 
them. Students who wish may also use a state-of-the-art proof construction tutor 
now being developed at CMU. Grading is based on how far one gets in the 
computer's curriculum and on a final exam.
COURSE: Gander Issues In Moral and Political
EhllPSPPhv (80-234)
TIME: TR 12:30-1:50
PLACE: SH 206
INSTRUCTOR: Ernie Alieva
COURSE DESCRIPTION:
Gender Issues in Moral and Political Philosophy
We will discuss various theoretical and applied issues concerning 
gender: recent feminist work and critical responses; research on 
gender and moral development/moral reasoning; conceptions of 
masculinity, femininity and androgyny; interpretations of gender 
equality and difference; reproductive and childrearing issues; 
gender issues regarding work; gay/lesbian rights; proposals for 
legal/institutional change.
COURSE: 80-237 PHILOSOPHY OF LAW
TIME: MW 11-12:20
PLACE: SH220
INSTRUCTOR: LISA FRANK
COURSE DESCRIPTION:
This course will consider a variety of isues in the philosophy of 
law. Attention will be given to competing theories of law as well as to 
substantive descriptive and normative quiestions which advocates of these 
theoires propose to answer. Central to our readings will be the problem (or set 
of problems) of the relation between law and politics/ethics. We will attempt to 
identify and evaluate the assumptions about political/ethical reasoning 
operative in the competing philosophies of law.
Students will be required to do a substantial amount of reading as well as write 
three papers. There is no mid-term or final exam. This format presupposes 
students self-discipline and those who do not have it are encouraged not to 
enroll.
COURSE REQUIREMENTS:
. Class participation (10% of grade)
. One or more in-class presentations of a research project (30%)
. Completion of a research report on a project in natural-language 
processing, including demonstration of a running system (60%)
COURSE: TOPICS IN THE HISTORY OF PHILOS. (80-250)
TIME: TR 1-:20
PLACE: MMA14
INSTRUCTOR: D CARRIER
COURSE DESCRIPTION:
This course will concern itself with the relationship between a Philosopher's 
Arguments and Literary style of his works.This question has recently been 
discussed within and outside this University. Analytic philosophers as well as 
their colleagues in 'continental' philosophy have experimented with a variety of 
different ways of presenting an argument. We will be concerned to do a close 
reading of a number of texts which deal with this problem. We begin with two 
classics nineteenth century accounts, Kieregaard's Fear and Trembling and 
Nietzsche's On the Genealogy of Morals. We then turn to the early and late 
masterpieces of Wittgenstein. His early Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus present 
a highly complex, very influential argument which is criticized in his late 
Philosophical Investigations. We read, also a novel by another influential 
modem philosopher which offers a concise presentation of part of his more 
technical arguments, Sartre's Nausea. Finally, we turn to two recent much 
discussed works. Arthur Danto's The Transfiguration of the Commonplace and 
Deleuze and Guattari's Anti-Oedipus. Written in very different styles, these two 
works are both concerned with finding novel ways of presenting philosophical 
arguments.
Our primary interest will be in discussing our reading of these 
difficult tests. There are two papers and three exams. The course does not 
presuppose any particular background in philosophy. There is no final.

an account of Manet's early Moderism. We conclude the analysis of painting 
with various theories of abstract art, and a brief view of the recently fashionable 
'postmodernism'. We will be interested in seeing how paintings from different

COURSE:LOGIC ANP ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE
80-311/711)
TIME: TR 2-3:20
PLACE: A18A
INSTRUCTOR: KEVIN KELLY
COURSE DESCRIPTION:
When you write an artificial intelligence program, you are 
frequently challenged with the question whether there is anything particularly 
interesting about the program you have written. It is one way to do what it does 
Maybe some other way is better. To address such questions, you must specify 
clearly what it is that your program is supposed to do, and then one must show 
that it does it. Morever, to show that your program is an interesting one, it is 
important to be able to argue that no other program could do much better.
Artificial Intelligence programs have to do broadly with 
reasoning. Problems concerning reasoning are often most clearly specified as 
logical problems. This is no surprise, since logic is the study of norms or 
reasoning. In this course, we will use logic as a tool to assist us in the 
understanding and assessment of artificial intelligence systems.
The course will focus on two major topics in artificial 
intelligence: machine learning and the frame probiem. Machine leamina 
intends to find good programs for making a computer smarter in light of past 
experience with a problem. We will examine a range of machine learning 
programs and we will analyze them from a logical point of view. Programs to be 
reviewed include Winston's concept learning system, Herbert Simons BACON 
program, version space" algorithms and various proposals for explanationbased learning". Underlying our assessment of these programs will be a 
theoretical framework for program assessment known as formal learning theory, 
which applies logical and computational concepts to questions about what is 
learnable by machines.
The frame problem arises when something changes, and we 
have to re-check everything else to see whether or not it changed. A solution to 
the frame problem would be a computerized logical system that doesnt have to 
check certain things to know that they havent changed. We will examine 
approaches to the frame problem, including McCarthy's notion of 
circumscription" and various non-monotonic logics" to see how they address 
the problem.
PREREQUISITES: A course In mathematical logic and some 
programming ability or permmlsion of Instructor,.
BOURSE: Moral Problems and the World of Work
(80-341)
TIME: TR 2:30-3:50
PLACE: SH 206
INSTUCTOR: Ernie Alieva
COURSE DESCRIPTION:
Moral Problems and the World of Work
We will examine a variety of normative issues: rights and 
obligations regarding work; employment opportunities; 
remuneration and other work-related benefits; power and authority 
in the workplace; occupational health and safety; gender differences 
at work; proposals for workplace reforms; etc. We will also address 
related philosophical and empirical issues: historical and crosscultural perspectives on work; individual and social meanings of 
work; the division of labor; automation/technological change; 
labor/leisure and efficiency/quality of working life trade-offs; 
alternative forms of workplace organization; the role of unions; 
workers' political movements.
COURSE:LOGIC ft COMPUTABILITY II (80-410)
TIME: TR 10-11:20
PLACE: PH 100
INSTRUCTOR: WILFRIED SIEG
COURSE DESCRIPTION:
This is a continuation of Logic & Computability 1(80-310); but it 
can be taken independently by students who are familiar with the syntax of 
predicate logic. The first part of the course is concerned with the semantics of 
. predicate logic and presents basic metamathematical results, such as Godel's 
Completeness Theorems, Compactness Theorem, Lowenheim-Skolem 
Theorems, and Herbrands Theorem. The second part focusses on Godel's 
Incompleteness Theorems and presents in detail the requisite notions and 
results of computability theory, i.e., recursive functions and representability.
PRE-REQ- PERMISSION OF INS.RUCTOR IS REQUIRED.
COURSE: NATURAL LANGUAGE PROCESSING I (8Q-.4BU
TIME:
PLACE:
INSTRUCTOR:
TR 10:30-11:50 
BH 255A
BOB CARPENTER
COURSE DESCRIPTION:
This is the first in a series of three courses in natural language 
processing. The NLP 1-111 sequence is meant to develop an in-depth 
understanding of both the algorithms available for the processing of linguistic 
information and the underlying computational properties of natural languages.
NLP I is devoted to the study of phonological, morphological and 
syntactic processing. We will study both computational models of such 
processing and psychological studies of human processing.
We will introduce the notion of computational complexity and apply it 
to the study of parsing algorithms for both syntactic and morphological 
information,
We will also spend a good deal of time on unification algorithms and 
the processing of partial information in unification grammars as well as the 
ability of such grammars to represent grammatical information.
In the last part of the class, we will introduce some connectionist and 
paramter-based models of parsing and compare their properties to those of 
more traditional phrase-structure models of grammar.
NLP II will then study semantic and pragmatic processing algorithms, 
while NLP III is devouied to the construction of a project.

COURSE: NATURAL-LANGUAGE PROCESSING III
_(NLP:lllK8Q.-483)
TIME: TR 10:30-11:50
PLACE: PH125B
INSTRUCTOR: JAIME CARBONELL
COURSE DESCRIPTION:
This course is intended to be the final course in a sequence of three 
courses intoducing the student to fundamental principles and applications in 
contemporary computational linguistics and natural-language processing 
theory. As such, it is designed to give the student an opportunity to. apply the 
lessons of the first two courses in pursuing a semester-long research project 
under the supervsion of a research scientist-in particular, to modify, design, or 
implement components in natural-language processing systems.
The course will be conducted as a research seminar; there will be no 
formal lectures. During the first weeks of the course, faculty and research 
scientists will make presentations on selected research topics, largely to 
familiarize the class with the context of the work that individual students will 
attempt. In the middle weeks of the course, students will describe their plans for 
research projects, focusing especially on the issues for computational 
linguistics and NLP. In the last part of the course, students will be expected to 
make full research reports on their accomplishments. At times during the 
semester, the course may meet less frequently than the twice-per week class 
schedule.
To participate in the class, students must secure research 
supervisors. The course is designed to bring together individual students and 
research scientists in the Carnegie Mellon/University of Pittsburgh community 
for work on a long-term project with a relatively narrow focus. It would be most 
beneficial for each student to seek research-project supervision from a scientist 
working in an area of the student's interests. However, should students not 
have particular supervisors or projects in mind, the course instructors will act as 
default supervisors and will direct students to projects and topics that will satisfy 
the requirements of the course.
As one of their required class presentations, students will be 
expected to arrange for a class presentation by their project supervisors. At a 
later point in the semester, students will lead a class seminar with their 
supervisors present, at which both the student and supervisor provide the class 
with information about the studnet's plan for a project. It will be the student's 
responsibility to coordinate seminars with the supervisor.
Students will be evaluated both on their oral and written reports (on 
their projects) and on the basis of a report from their project supervisors.
COURSE: CONNECTIONIST APPROACHES TO
LlNGUISTICS(80-487)
TIME: TR 3:00-4:20
PLACE: WEH 5403
INSTRUCTOR: DAVID EVANS(BH 135L, x8574)
DAVID TOURETZKY(WEH 8125, X7561 )
COURSE DESCRIPTION:
This course is designed to introduce the student to the principles of 
connectionist modeling as applied to linguistic phenomena. The course will 
offer background material on connectionism and specific linguistic problems in 
areas such as phonology, morphology, and syntax, as well as a discussion of 
current connectionist research in linguistics. In addition, we will discuss issues 
such as the reality of rules and symbols in connectionist systems; general 
approaches to handling sequences and structured objects in neural nets; 
representations developed by connectionist learning algorithms; and child 
language acquisition.
The course will be conducted principally as a lecture course 
with some aspects of a seminar. There will be readings, possible student 
presentations in class, and programming assignements.
COURSE REQUIREMENTS:
. Class participation (10% of grade)
. Completion of Programming exercises and assignments (90%)
REQUIRED TEXTS.
None. Papers will be made available.*This course is also offered as 15-880, Advanced Topics in Al. Questions 
should be directed to Maria Intrieri in CS.
COURSE: COMP. LING. THEORY SEMINAR80-685)
TIME: TR 1:30-2:50
PLACE: PH A22
INSTRUCTOR: BOB CARPENTER
COURSE DESCRIPTION:
This is the third in a series of three courses in the theory of 
computation focusing on computational linguistics issues.
During the first half of the course we will develop the theory of 
computational complexity. We will analyze the complexity of algorithms using 
techniques from combinatorics. We will also analyze the complexity of 
problems in terms of the best available algorithms. In particular, we will develop 
the theory of NP completeness and spend a good deal of time on the concept of 
reduction and relative computation. We will also study the speed-up possible 
with parallel processing.
The second half of the course will be devoted to the study of 
the semantics of computer programs. We will develop enough domain theory to 
be able to carry out the development of a denotational semantics for both typed 
and untyped functional languages. This will involve the study of the abstract 
properties of unification.
We will also spend'time during the course covering 
background material drawn from set theory, algebra, logic and computability. 
We will not be following a single textbook, but references will be provided.
Grading will be based on weekly problem sets.
Formal Pre-reqs: Logic, Formal Languages and Automata
TIME: MW 2:30-3:50
PLACE: PHA19A
INSTRUCTOR: TEDDY SEIDENFELD
COURSE DESCRIPTION:
We shall study foundational questions in expected utility theory-questions 
dealing with the axiomatization, representation, and measurement of probability 
and utilitywith attention to applications in statistical decision theory. Also, we 
shall contrast several departures from expected utility. These include economic 
and psychological theories of preference without the independence" (Savages
Sure-Thing) axiom and theories which do not require comparisons (ordering")
between all pairs of acts. To what extent do these departures from expected 
utility theory provide a rationale for classical (non-Bayesian) statistical 
practices?
Primary Text: The Foundations of Statistics(2nd. revised d.). LJ. Savage 
Secondary material will be provided.
Requirements:
Students will be expected to make a class presentation and will be 
required to write a term paper on a topic chosen in consultation with the 
instructor.

A Persons Opinion
The Central Intelligence 
Agency visited Carnegie Mellon 
Thursday to interview twenty 
four students searching forjobs.
However, the group's presence 
on campus was met with some 
resistance. Protestors came out 
in force when more than thirty 
peopleincluding faculty,staff, 
and studentspicketed in front 
of Warner Hall. The signs they 
carried demanded that the CIA 
be banned from recruiting on 
campus.
The event was staged by 
Educational Action on Latin 
America. The group came out to 
protest the agencys use of UniY versity facilities to recruit stu
dents for employment and to 
raise the public awarness of 
questionable episodes of CIA 
intervention abroad.
Speakers explained why they 
thought the CIA should not reS cruit on campus while partici
pants distributed literature 
about the questionalble activi
ties of the agency.
We asked several members of
 the campus what they thought 
. about CIA recruitment at CMU.
Lisa Katz 
Sophomore IM

The Tartan  Carnegie Mellon  31 October 1989L3
Halloween is the oldest and perhaps the most loved holiday of them all
HP4I4 vb zyanI,tr 8nrp,In
Pictured in front of Morewood Gardens are four Halloween enthusiasts 
preparing to participate in the age old tradition of trick-or-treating.
s4aI,atlg from Hnyl Gj
saint, was meant to substitute for 
Samhain, draw off the devotion of 
the Celtic peoples, and finally 
replace it forever.
But the old beliefs were simply 
too strong to die out, probably 
because of the powerful symbol
ism of the traveling dead. People 
couldnt replace the terrifying 
Samhain with the abstract holi
day of All Saints. The Church 
realized this a few hundred years 
later and in the ninth century 
tried once again to replace 
Samhain.
This time the Church estab
lished November 2 as All Souls 
Day. On this day, the living prayed 
for the souls of the dead. How
ever, the Churchs new holiday 
had a reverse effect. Instead of 
the All Saints Day, otherwise 
known as All Hallows, weaken
ing the Celtic beliefs, the Church 
just intensified them. People 
continued to celebrate All Hal
lows Eve as a time of the wander
ing dead, but the supernatural 
beings were now associated with 
evil. From there on in, All Hal- lowsEve, alias Hallow Even, alias
Halloween, became embedded 
into our culture.
Virtually all the traditions as
sociated with Halloween today 
can be traced back to Samhain. 
The wearing of costumes can be 
traced to the first few centuries of 
Christianity, when it was believed 
that along with the souls of the 
dead were fairies, witches, and 
demons. People used to dress up 
like the spirits so that they would 
be tricked into thinking that the 
humans were actually one of 
them.
Going from door to door and 
asking for treats has many ori
gins. During Celtic times, chil
dren used to go around begging 
for soul-cakes, a special treat 
made only on Samhain.
During the first few centuries 
of Christianity, food and drink 
were left out to placate the dead. 
As the centimes continued, people 
began to perform antics in ex
change for offerings of food and 
drink. This practice was called 
mumming and eventually evolved 
into our present day trick-or-treating.
Another hallmark of Hallow
een is the jack-o-lantem. During 
the time of the Celtics, jack-olantems were used to light the 
way for wandering souls and the 
faces were carved in them to scare 
away demons and witches. In 
America, Jack is always carved in 
a pumpkin, but in Great Britain 
and Ireland, where the custom 
originated, turnips are the favored 
vegetable.
As the story goes, a blacksmith 
named Jack made an agreement 
with the Devil. The agreement 
was that he would give up his soul 
in return for seven years of abso
lute mastery of his trade. After he 
made the deal Jack hung a sign 
outside his shop which proudly 
proclaimed, Here lies the Master 
of all Masters.
One day, Saint Peter and Christ 
himself paid Jack a visit. They 
were disturbed by the sign and 
tried to instill some humility in 
Jack by working a few miracles. 
However, Christ did not succeed. 
Saint Peter then offered to grant 
Jack three wishes. Jacks first 
wish was that whenever he told 
someone to climb a nearby pear 
tree that person would have to 
stay in the tree until Jack allowed 
him to come down. Saint Peter 
saw this wish as odd, but said 
nothing. Jack made the same 
wishes with his remaining two 
regarding his armchair and his 
purse: one must stay in them until 
Jack allowed the person to go.
Saint Peter then said to Jack, 
You have wished very foolishly. 
You should have wished for ever
lasting peace in Heaven.
Nevertheless, Jack used these 
three wishes to trick the Devil 
when he came for Jacks soul. 
Each time the Devil came Jack 
managed to trick him into climb
ing the tree, sitting in his chair, 
and shrinking himself and enter
ing Jacks purse. Each time the 
Devil gave Jack seven years in
return for his freedom. In the end 
the Devil just fled in terror.
Eventually on Jacks time fi
nally came. First Jack went to the 
Pearly Gates, but Saint Peter did 
not allow him into Heaven. So to 
Hell Jack went, but only to find 
that the Devil wanted nothing to 
do with him.
Ive had enough of this one 
already, the Devil said. Youll 
not torment me here.
The Devil then ordered all nine 
locks of Hell bolted, but before 
Jack was shut out entirely he
managed to scoop up a burning 
coal from the fires of Hell in the 
turnip he was eating. To this day 
Jack uses this lantern to light 
his way as he wanders the earth 
until Judgment Day.
The holiday and its traditions 
was originally brought to Amer
ica in the nineteenth century by a 
great number of Irish immigrants. 
From this period on, numerous 
other traditions have arisen as 
well, such as snap-apple (trying 
to catch an apple in your mouth 
which is suspended from the ceil
ing) and the burning of nuts 
(according to the way they bum, 
the initials of ones future spouse 
can be read in the ashes). Hallow
een is a holiday which continu
ally passes on one tradition after 
another. j
Of all the major holidays ^we 
celebrate, Halloween is probably 
the oldest and without a doubt 
the oddest. But even after almost 
four thousand years of changes, 
Halloween is imbedded into our 
lives and is still celebrated a a 
holy and magical night for all.
The Barbershop Quartet gives 
high notes to Enricos and Cimini
By MICHAEL T. ROSE
Tartan Diversions Editor 
& STEPHEN GLICKER
Tartan Asst. Diversions Editor
Editors note: This is the first 
of a two part series, in which 
four people go out and try to find 
the best place to get a haircut in 
the Pittsburgh area.
Enricos
When you go to Enricos, on 
Meyran Avenue in Oakland, you 
have a unique tonsorial deci
sion to make  junior or sen
ior? Enricos is a father-son op
eration, and the cut you get can 
dep end on the generation of your 
barber.
The shop, a blue-awninged 
storefront just a few steps down 
from Fifth, has a funky and 
laid-back atmosphere. The 
waiting room, partitioned off 
from the three-chair business 
end of the shop by a wicker wall, 
is decorated with hanging 
plants. It features a wide selec
tion of fashion and news maga
zines, which one may peruse while 
lounging on what appear to be re
conditioned automobile seats.
Enricos accepts both reserva
tions and walk-ins, so I opted to 
test the traffic by arriving with
out calling first. I was in the chair 
within ten minutes, and since 
Enricos is open until 6 pm, you 
can pick a time that fits your busy 
schedule.
Since Enrico Sr. had the open 
chair, I took my cut from him. The 
elder barber has a gentle, cau
tious style, preferring to make 
several small snips instead of 3 
large single cut. Hes also ofth0 
sit-and-spin school; he re-1 
mains seated on a high stool 
while rotating the customers 
chair to get at those hard-toreach spots  not for those 
easily susceptible to motion 
sickness.
Enrico Junior is a bit more 
flamboyant than his father^ 
Dressed all in black, he sing^

Part of Uncle Edgars memoirs are reprinted for the first time ever
By JASON LEE SMITH
Tartan Staffwriter
One of the most vivid figures 
from my childhood is my Uncle 
Edgar. Even in his eighties he 
was a strong, firm man. He was a 
man of the world, a man who had 
taken the worst life had to offer, 
but still did not break. How well 
I remember him wandering 
through our house in his wheel
chair and muttering incoherently 
to himself.
I was his favorite and only 
nephew, and he loved to tell me 
stories. I used to sit enraptured 
for hours as he would tell me 
stories of his life. He told me about 
fighting the Turks in Crete, and 
the Cretins in Turkey. He told me 
how he had been an astronaut. He 
told me of his secret adventures in 
the CIA, and of how he personally 
brought John F. Kennedys killer 
to justice.
He was a filthy liar, but I loved 
him.
Recently I was rummaging 
through the attic, looking for my 
cufflinks, and I happened to find 
my uncles memoirs. They were 
coated with dust and the pages 
were yellow with age, but then so 
was my uncle. It is with great 
pleasure that I reprint parts of 
them here.
I hope they will provide as much 
inspiration to you as they have to 
me.
You might say Ive been around 
a bit. Ive traveled to the four 
corners of the earth, and some 
parts of Norway. Ive seen some 
of the marvels of the universe. 
And throughout my wanderings, 
whats my motto been? KnowlRESEARCH PAPERS

Cruise Ship Jobs
zqzF3 Ala f L4pla2 8tpplu( >lnu q4tagS UVdV3qKUwq8S dV9q 39zTw8S qwOqwKdzVF Uwq8VFFwGS w0slMMlaI Hnb HMtr hqww IunolMS Onu,vvlna2 nBn,,2 EnPnpnrS 84tIP Uns,i,s2 Al0,s4S 
OKGG FVL OnMM ulitagnvMlS 
:fme?fj?feS w0IS ?ee FS
edge, always knowledge....
Ever since I can remember, Ive 
been on a quest for the meaning of 
existence. Its been a search which 
has taken me most of my life. How 
well I remember the first time I 
questioned the nature of God. I 
posed the question, If God exists 
in only a spiritual and therefore 
theoretical form, how are we 
supposed to view the metamor
phosis from youth to old age to 
death? My kindergarten teacher 
didnt know.
That day, after nap-time, I 
began my journey.
It has been a trip which has 
lasted four decades and has cov
ered sixty-seven continents. (I
went back and visited a lot of 
them twice.) Ive spoken with 
Brahmans in India, Buddhist
Monks in the Orient, and a Ko
sher Butcher in New Jersey.
And after all my work  noth
ing.
Thats not to say I havent been 
close. Many frustrating times Ive 
been painfully close to the ulti
mate revelation, when it has been 
snatched out of my hands by cruel 
Fate.
Once, in Iraq, I met a priest who 
promised to reveal to me the se
cret of eternal life, but he died 
before he could tell me.
A scholar I knew in China once 
said, You should never go any
where without warming over the 
gravy, and be sure to smell the 
pajamas before the pygmies
come. He was a psychopath, 
true, but he had a knowledge 
which we could never fathom, here 
in the West.
My motto has been knowl
edge, always knowledge and I 
have done my best to live by that 
motto and gather whatever knowl
edge I could. In the mountains of 
Tibet, a wiseman befriended me. 
He taught me how to deaden, my 
ego, project my spirit, and how to 
prevent getting waxy build-up on 
my furniture.
In India, I heard of a monk who 
was reported to know the secret of 
the universe, but, alas, he had 
taken a vow of silence. I followed 
him for seven days, trying to
persuade him to give me some 
hint. It seemed hopeless, bu^ I 
persevered, and on the eighth day 
my efforts were rewarded. He bent 
down, and knelt close to my e^tr, 
and, in a voice unused to speecjh, 
told me to bug off....
The life of the adventurer is n(ot 
an easy one, but I will persevere. 
As I trudge through all the slimy, 
muck-encrusted cesspools of 
humanity looking for all that-is 
good and beautiful in the world, I 
will continue to live by my motto: 
knowledge, always knowl
edge.
Jason Lee Smith is a freshman 
in CIT.

here practicing on the CFA lawn, is a medieval and Renaissance 
reenactment group. They also practice dancing and singing from 
the Middle Ages on Thursdays at 8:30 pm in Porter Hall 225C.

The Glenn Miller Orchestra 
plays this Friday from 9 pm to 1 
am in the Skibo Ballroom, with 
free dance lessons from 8 pm to 9 
pm. The event is an AB Dance, 
and is funded by your Activits 
Fee. Admission is free.
Right: Bandleader 
Glenn Miller, whose 
band popularized the 
swing sound of the 
1930s. Miller 
disappeared in 1944 
under mysterious 
circumstances.

The budgets for these organizations will be heard by the full Student Senate on Tuesday,

are invited to this hearing to express opinions about these organizations or their budgets. The 
Student Senate may use this information as it sees fit to revise the budget allocations.

SPEND YOUR I
We are pleased to announce that the Carla Schoen Institute 
of Ohr Somayach Jerusalem is once again sponsoring its 
"Winter Vacation in Israel study and touring program.
WHEN: The main 
program is from December 26, 1989 through 
January 8, 1990. There is an additional 
optional week until January 15. However, 
due to the wide variation in vacation 
schedules, arrangements can be made for 
students to arrive as early as December 
13, to leave as late as January 29 and to 
stay for 2-4 weeks during that period.
vmm
IN
ASLITTLEAS$250
WHAT:1. A comp
rehensive introduction to Talmudic 
analysis, Jewish history and philosophy, 
and contemporary 
theological, ethical and 
political issues taught by 
an internationallyrenowned staff of scholars.
2. Specially designed 
tours of Jerusalem, the 
Galilee, the Golan 
Heights, Masada and 
the Dead Sea.
WHERE: The pro
gram is based at 
the modern Jerusalem 
campus of Ohr Somayach.
WHO: Jewish men 
between the ages of 18-30 with limited 
backgrounds in Jewish studies, demonstrated 
academic achievement and a sincere 
motivation to explore their Jewish roots.
HOW MUCH: A
limited number of scholarships will be 
available which will reduce the total 
price to $250.
This includes round trip airfare 
from New York, tuition, room and board
and tours.
A REPRESENTATIVE
will be available to discuss the program on
Monday, Nov. 6 at Hillel Lounge. 160 N. Craig St.
4-7:30 PM. For further INFORMATION
and an application contact the Winter Vacation Scholarship 
Program at Ohr Somayach, 39 Broadway, New York, New York 
10006, 212-785-1550 or 800-431-2272.

The Tartan  Carnegie Mellon  31 October 1989L15
pnoto courtesy Ubb Kecoras 
A still from the animated segments of Pink Floyd's The Wall.
The Ex. NoMeansNo. and Whistle elav tonight, so...
nMM4Blla u4sJr nI IPl 9HrInyl
By DAN BIDWA
Tartan Senior Staffwriter
The treats are down the street 
tonight. The Ex, NoMeansNo, and 
Whistle are playing at the Up
stage at 7:30 pm. Dont miss it.
Why not? Well, for a few differ
ent reasons. First of all, Whistle 
is one of the up-and-coming-est 
bands in Pittsburgh. The band 
includes Damon Che from Half 
Life and the Heretics, Matt 
Marcus from Crow Flies, Bbronk, 
and a bunch of other bands, just 
to drop a few names. Theyre very 
good, theyve gotten better every 
time Ive seen them, and youll 
regret it later if you miss them 
now (before they change their 
name to something a little less 
copyrighted).
Secondly, NoMeansNo is one of 
Canadas best bands. Most people 
equate Canadian rock with Corey 
Hart or Loverboy, so think of 
NoMeansNo as a learning experi
ence. Youre in college, these 
things happen.
Since D.O.A. died, NoMeansNo 
is probably Canadas best known 
punk band, and for good reason. 
Theyve got a batch of vinyl out, 
with their latest release, Wrong, 
set to appear any time now. All of 
them show off the bands ability 
to combine the velocity of hardcore 
(sometimes) with the bass lines of 
funk (sometimes) to end up with 
something more interesting than 
either. Top it all off with lyrics far 
above the I hate you, you suck 
brand ofpunk expressionism, and 
youve got a learning experience.
Music...
Music...
review
Really, theyre very good.
The final, and major reason for 
showing up at the Upstage to
night is The Ex. Theyre Dutch, 
they have seven LPs and a batch 
of seven inchers out (mostly un
available in the U.S., except for 
their last four LPs), and this is 
their first time in the U.S. The Ex 
is a punk band in the purest sense 
of the word  they started the 
band (five people playing guitars, 
bass, drums) because they liked 
making music. Their music is 
speedy, abrasive, fascinating, 
intelligent, and firmly rooted in 
the Crass-Flux-Chumbawamba 
-Mekons school of leftist anar
chism. (Its just about a given fact 
that articles from lame writers 
describing The Ex will mention 
Crass at some point. Sorry.)
Why politics? In an essay en
titled Into The Gravy  from 
Threat By Example, a collection 
of essays compiled by Martin 
Sprouse that you might want to 
hunt for  G.W. Sok, the bands 
frontman, put it like this: We 
believe that any kind of cultural 
expression reflects a political 
choice. Either one of mindless, 
stupefying escapism from reality, 
or one of happiness and anger.
When we know 
that a block away 
a house is getting 
evicted [sic], the 
cops beat up people 
for no reason, or a 
couple of business
men are kicking 
large numbers of 
employees onto the 
street, then were simply not in 
the mood to sing silly love songs.
The idea of a band actually 
giving a shit is so foreign to the 
entire music industry these days 
that finding one that does, and 
does so passionately, is wonder
ful and ultimately inspiring. I 
dont know how to put it any more 
succinctly. If you have ever 
wanted to get involved but 
havent, this is your chance, be
cause The Ex is involved. You 
should get involved with The Ex.
The show starts at 7:30 pm, 
costs $7, and is open to all ages.
Later this week: Bullet LaVolta 
returns to Pittsburgh. Theyll be 
playing at the Upstage along with 
Jawbox, Liquid Boy, and the 
Heretics this Friday.
Last seen at the Sonic Temple, 
Bullet LaVolta has since released 
its first full LP, called The Gift, on 
Taang. They subsequently 
snagged a contract with RCA, who 
have rereleased The Gift in its 
entirety, along with a couple of 
extra tracks added from Bullet 
LaVoltas debut EP. As a result, 
this tour is a much bigger deal 
than last summers.
Ordinarily, all this sudden at
tention would be the kiss of death
for a four-year-old band like Bul
let LaVolta, but not this time, at 
least not immediately. Since The 
Gift was recorded before all the 
major label attention came to 
roost, and since RCA hasnt 
mucked with the production, the 
record packs more of a punch than 
anything else released by RCA in 
the past five years. The bands 
new material, first heard this 
summer in New York, follows the 
same style as established on the 
LP  noisy rock and roll, punk 
rock played at a median speed 
that alienates yuppies and skin
heads alike, best heard live.
Jawbox and Liquid Boy are both 
bands born from the ashes of 
Government Issue. When G.I. 
broke up, guitarist Tom Lyle went 
back to dentistry school (he was 
almost finished when Crash came 
out), singer John Stabb formed a 
band called Weatherhead, and 
drummer Peter Moffett and bas
sist Jay Robbins formed Jawbox 
and Liquid Boy, respectively. Both 
are rumored to sound remotely 
like G.I. (Robbins bought Tom 
Lyles trademark Marshall stack), 
but youll just have to see for 
yourself. Jawbox and Liquid Boy 
will be opening for Bullet LaVolta 
at the 7:30 pm all-ages show. The 
opening slot for the over-21 show 
at 11:30 pm will be filled by the 
Heretics. Admission will be $6 for 
the all-ages show and $5 for the 
over-21 show. The Upstage is at 
3609 Forbes Avenue.
Dan Bidwa is a junior writing 
major and the music director of 
WRCT.
dPldunolMlur&
You're better off under the Umbrella
TheTravelers Companies. Hartford, Connecticut 06183. An Equal Opportunity Employer.
Thursday, November 2
Wish You Were Here
Lynda likes to show boys her legs! No big deal, 
Well, in 1951 in a working class neighborhood 
of an English provincial town, it was a big deal. Wish 
You Were Here tells the story of Lyndas adolescence 
as she tries to express her self in a very closed-minded 
society. No one seems to understand her despite her 
good nature and fun-loving spirit. This factor is pres
ent throughout the movie, making it much more 
interesting. Emily Lloyd stars as Lynda and gives a 
very good performance.
. Not Rated  At 6, 8, 10
TELECOMMUNICATIONS
If you think you've come far in the past four years, 
you won't believe how far you'll go in the next five.
Friday, November 3
Pink Floyd  The Wall
" Although many people, including myself, never 
realized there was a plan to The Wall, there is one 
, indeed. Pink, played by Bob Geldof, is a British rock 
star, sitting in his Los Angeles hotel room, feeling the 
I worlds burden on his shoulders. He daydreams about 
warfare, his mother, childhood, the wife who left him, 
and fascism. These daydreams take up a large part of 
the movie. If you are the highly analytical type, you 
might have a hard time putting everything together 
in your normal state of mind. This movie, like it or not, 
is an experience you should not be without. The music 
and animation by themselves are great and make the 
experience worthwhile evenifyou dont like the movie. 
If you havent already seen this movie, do not miss it 
again.
Rated RAt 6, 8,10, 12
Saturday, November 4
Running On Empty
Running on Empty is the story of a family on the 
ran. The parents, played by Judd Hirsch and Christine 
Lahti, are trying to escape their past  they tried to 
blow up a chemical plant in the 1960s. After many 
new names, towns, and jobs they finally get a little 
settled somewhere. Wouldnt you know it  the son 
(River Phoenix) falls in love with his piano teachers 
daughter (Martha Plimpton). The son has a talent for 
the piano, and after much persuasion he applies to 
Julliard. He is accepted, and this sets up the conflict. 
To attend school would break up the family because 
he would stop running while his family could not. 
Running On Empty is a touching exploration of the re
lationships in an unusual American family.
Rated PG-13  At 6:30, 9, 11:30
Sunday, November 5
Goldfinger
This 1964 release stars the original, best Bond, 
Sean Connery. In this adventure, 007 deals with his 
normal share of bad guys, attractive women, and 
wonderful gadgets in a way only James Bond could. 
Gert Frobe plays the villain, Goldfinger, who has his 
own evil plan for the world. It involves controlling all 
the worlds supply of  you guessed it  gold. The 
finale is classic Bond and involves Goldfingers at
tempt to contaminate all the gold in Fort Knox with a 
nuclear bomb. Sounds typical, but something makes 
Goldfinger better than the norm of007 flicks. There is 
no doubt that this is a great Bond adventure and 
worth your time and money.
Not Rated  At 6, 8, 10, 12
By JASON DIGMAN
If you've spent the past four years 
developing a solid technical background, we 
have an extraordinary opportunity for your 
next five: The Travelers Telecommunications 
Technology Management Program (TTMP).
The most comprehensive training pro
gram of its kind in the financial services indus
try, TTMP is a five-year introduction to a 
state-of-the-art telecommunications environ
ment. Through a variety of rotational assign
ments-and supplemental classroom study 
TTMP is singly focused on developing the man
agers who will keep us on the cutting edge.
TTMP is not easy and it's not for every
one. But, if you're looking to develop the full 
range of skills needed to manage the most 
sophisticated telecommunications technology 
available, there's simply no better place to 
begin your career.
Find out more.
Come to The Travelers' Presentation: 
Wednesday, November 1st at 7pm,
Skibo Center, Room 12.
Casual dress. Refreshments will be served.
